<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Walter Scott</h1>			<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
				<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>				<div id="contentSub"></div>
				<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>				<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
				<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
				<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">18th/19th-century Scottish historical novelist, poet and playwright </div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">For other people named Walter Scott, see <a href="/wiki/Walter_Scott_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Walter Scott (disambiguation)">Walter Scott (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<table class="infobox vcard" style="width:22em"><tbody><tr><th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;font-size:125%;font-weight:bold"><span class="fn">Sir Walter Scott, Bart.</span></th></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">
<a href="/wiki/File:Sir_Henry_Raeburn_-_Portrait_of_Sir_Walter_Scott.jpg" class="image" title="Raeburn&#39;s portrait of Sir Walter Scott in 1822"><img alt="Raeburn&#39;s portrait of Sir Walter Scott in 1822" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Sir_Henry_Raeburn_-_Portrait_of_Sir_Walter_Scott.jpg/250px-Sir_Henry_Raeburn_-_Portrait_of_Sir_Walter_Scott.jpg" width="250" height="303" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Sir_Henry_Raeburn_-_Portrait_of_Sir_Walter_Scott.jpg/375px-Sir_Henry_Raeburn_-_Portrait_of_Sir_Walter_Scott.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Sir_Henry_Raeburn_-_Portrait_of_Sir_Walter_Scott.jpg/500px-Sir_Henry_Raeburn_-_Portrait_of_Sir_Walter_Scott.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1649" data-file-height="2000" /></a><div><a href="/wiki/Henry_Raeburn" title="Henry Raeburn">Raeburn</a>'s portrait of Sir Walter Scott in 1822</div></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding-top:0.225em;line-height:1.1em;padding-right:0.65em;">Born</th><td style="line-height:1.4em;">

Scottish</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding-top:0.225em;line-height:1.1em;padding-right:0.65em;">Alma&#160;mater</th><td style="line-height:1.4em;">
<a href="/wiki/University_of_Edinburgh" title="University of Edinburgh">University of Edinburgh</a></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding-top:0.225em;line-height:1.1em;padding-right:0.65em;">Period</th><td style="line-height:1.4em;">

<a href="/wiki/File:Sir_Walter_Scott_Signature.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Sir_Walter_Scott_Signature.jpg/160px-Sir_Walter_Scott_Signature.jpg" width="160" height="57" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Sir_Walter_Scott_Signature.jpg/240px-Sir_Walter_Scott_Signature.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Sir_Walter_Scott_Signature.jpg/320px-Sir_Walter_Scott_Signature.jpg 2x" data-file-width="892" data-file-height="319" /></a></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Sir Walter Scott, 1st Baronet</b> <span class="noexcerpt" style="font-size:85%;"><a href="/wiki/Fellow_of_the_Royal_Society_of_Edinburgh" title="Fellow of the Royal Society of Edinburgh">FRSE</a></span> (15 August 1771 – 21 September 1832) was a Scottish historical novelist, poet, playwright and historian. Many of his works remain classics of both English-language literature and of <a href="/wiki/Scottish_literature" title="Scottish literature">Scottish literature</a>. Famous titles include <i><a href="/wiki/Ivanhoe" title="Ivanhoe">Ivanhoe</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Rob_Roy_(novel)" title="Rob Roy (novel)">Rob Roy</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Old_Mortality" title="Old Mortality">Old Mortality</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Lady_of_the_Lake_(poem)" title="The Lady of the Lake (poem)">The Lady of the Lake</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Waverley_(novel)" title="Waverley (novel)">Waverley</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Heart_of_Midlothian" title="The Heart of Midlothian">The Heart of Midlothian</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Bride_of_Lammermoor" title="The Bride of Lammermoor">The Bride of Lammermoor</a></i>.
</p><p>Although primarily remembered for his extensive literary works and his political engagement, Scott was an <a href="/wiki/Advocate" title="Advocate">advocate</a>, judge and legal administrator by profession, and throughout his career combined his writing and editing work with his daily occupation as <a href="/wiki/Clerk_of_Session" class="mw-redirect" title="Clerk of Session">Clerk of Session</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sheriff_court" title="Sheriff court">Sheriff-Depute</a> of <a href="/wiki/Selkirkshire" title="Selkirkshire">Selkirkshire</a>.
</p><p>A prominent member of the <a href="/wiki/Tory_(political_faction)" class="mw-redirect" title="Tory (political faction)">Tory</a> establishment in Edinburgh, Scott was an active member of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Highland_and_Agricultural_Society_of_Scotland" title="Royal Highland and Agricultural Society of Scotland">Highland Society</a> and served a long term as President of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society_of_Edinburgh" title="Royal Society of Edinburgh">Royal Society of Edinburgh</a> (1820–32).
</p><p>Scott gathered the disparate strands of contemporary novel-writing techniques into his own hands and harnessed them to his deep interest in <a href="/wiki/Scottish_history" class="mw-redirect" title="Scottish history">Scottish history</a> and his knowledge of antiquarian lore. The technique of the <a href="/wiki/Omniscient_narrator" class="mw-redirect" title="Omniscient narrator">omniscient narrator</a> and the use of regional speech, localized settings, sophisticated character delineation, and romantic themes treated in a realistic manner were all combined by him into virtually a new literary form, the <a href="/wiki/Historical_novel" class="mw-redirect" title="Historical novel">historical novel</a>. His influence on other European and American novelists was immediate and profound, and though interest in some of his books declined somewhat in the <a href="/wiki/20th_century" title="20th century">20th century</a>, his reputation remains secure.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Life_and_works">Life and works</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Walter_Scott&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Life and works">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_days">Early days</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Walter_Scott&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Early days">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Smailholm_Tower_22407_from_E.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Smailholm_Tower_22407_from_E.jpg/220px-Smailholm_Tower_22407_from_E.jpg" width="220" height="152" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Smailholm_Tower_22407_from_E.jpg/330px-Smailholm_Tower_22407_from_E.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Smailholm_Tower_22407_from_E.jpg/440px-Smailholm_Tower_22407_from_E.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2161" data-file-height="1490" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Smailholm_Tower_22407_from_E.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Scott's childhood at Sandyknowes, in the shadow of <a href="/wiki/Smailholm_Tower" title="Smailholm Tower">Smailholm Tower</a>, introduced him to the tales and folklore of the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Borders" title="Scottish Borders">Scottish Borders</a>.</div></div></div><p> Walter Scott was born on 15 August 1771. He was the ninth child of Walter Scott, a <a href="/wiki/Society_of_Writers_to_Her_Majesty%27s_Signet" title="Society of Writers to Her Majesty&#39;s Signet">Writer to the Signet</a> (<a href="/wiki/Solicitor" title="Solicitor">solicitor</a>), and Anne Rutherford (sister of <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Rutherford" title="Daniel Rutherford">Daniel Rutherford</a>). His father was a member of a cadet branch of the Scotts Clan, and his mother descended from the Haliburton family, the descent from whom granted Walter's family the hereditary right of burial in <a href="/wiki/Dryburgh_Abbey" title="Dryburgh Abbey">Dryburgh Abbey</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> Via the Haliburton family, Walter (b.1771) was a cousin of the pre-eminent contemporaneous property developer <a href="/wiki/James_Burton_(property_developer)" title="James Burton (property developer)">James Burton</a>, who was a Haliburton who had shortened his surname, and of his son, the architect <a href="/wiki/Decimus_Burton" title="Decimus Burton">Decimus Burton</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BSLS_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BSLS-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Walter subsequently became a member of the <a href="/wiki/Clarence_Club" title="Clarence Club">Clarence Club</a>, of which the Burtons were also members.<sup id="cite_ref-Life_and_Letters_p.55_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Life_and_Letters_p.55-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Athenaeum_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Athenaeum-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Five of Walter's siblings died in infancy, and a sixth died when he was five months of age. Walter was born in a third-floor flat on College Wynd in the <a href="/wiki/Old_Town,_Edinburgh" title="Old Town, Edinburgh">Old Town</a> of <a href="/wiki/Edinburgh" title="Edinburgh">Edinburgh</a>, a narrow alleyway leading from the <a href="/wiki/Cowgate" title="Cowgate">Cowgate</a> to the gates of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Edinburgh" title="University of Edinburgh">University of Edinburgh</a> (Old College).<sup id="cite_ref-homes_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-homes-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> He survived a childhood bout of <a href="/wiki/Polio" class="mw-redirect" title="Polio">polio</a> in 1773 that left him lame,<sup id="cite_ref-T_E_Cone_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-T_E_Cone-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>  a condition that was to have a significant effect on his life and writing.<sup id="cite_ref-Robertson_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Robertson-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
To cure his lameness he was sent in 1773 to live in the rural <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Borders" title="Scottish Borders">Scottish Borders</a> at his paternal grandparents' farm at Sandyknowe, adjacent to the ruin of <a href="/wiki/Smailholm_Tower" title="Smailholm Tower">Smailholm Tower</a>, the earlier family home.<sup id="cite_ref-Sandy_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sandy-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Here he was taught to read by his aunt Jenny, and learned from her the speech patterns and many of the tales and legends that characterised much of his work. In January 1775 he returned to Edinburgh, and that summer went with his aunt Jenny to take spa treatment at <a href="/wiki/Bath,_Somerset" title="Bath, Somerset">Bath</a> in England, where they lived at 6 <a href="/wiki/South_Parade,_Bath" title="South Parade, Bath">South Parade</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> In the winter of 1776 he went back to Sandyknowe, with another attempt at a water cure at <a href="/wiki/Prestonpans" title="Prestonpans">Prestonpans</a> during the following summer.<sup id="cite_ref-Sandy_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sandy-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Scott%27s_family_home_in_George_Square,_Edinburgh.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/The_Scott%27s_family_home_in_George_Square%2C_Edinburgh.jpg/220px-The_Scott%27s_family_home_in_George_Square%2C_Edinburgh.jpg" width="220" height="330" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/The_Scott%27s_family_home_in_George_Square%2C_Edinburgh.jpg/330px-The_Scott%27s_family_home_in_George_Square%2C_Edinburgh.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/The_Scott%27s_family_home_in_George_Square%2C_Edinburgh.jpg/440px-The_Scott%27s_family_home_in_George_Square%2C_Edinburgh.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2056" data-file-height="3088" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Scott%27s_family_home_in_George_Square,_Edinburgh.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Scotts' family home in <a href="/wiki/George_Square,_Edinburgh" title="George Square, Edinburgh">George Square, Edinburgh</a></div></div></div>
<p>In 1778, Scott returned to Edinburgh for private education to prepare him for school, and joined his family in their new house built as one of the first in <a href="/wiki/George_Square,_Edinburgh" title="George Square, Edinburgh">George Square</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-homes_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-homes-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> In October 1779 he began at the <a href="/wiki/Royal_High_School_(Edinburgh)" class="mw-redirect" title="Royal High School (Edinburgh)">Royal High School of Edinburgh</a> (in High School Yards). He was now well able to walk and explore the city and the surrounding countryside. His reading included chivalric romances, poems, history and travel books. He was given private tuition by James Mitchell in arithmetic and writing, and learned from him the history of the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Scotland" title="Church of Scotland">Church of Scotland</a> with emphasis on the <a href="/wiki/Covenanters" class="mw-redirect" title="Covenanters">Covenanters</a>. After finishing school he was sent to stay for six months with his aunt Jenny in <a href="/wiki/Kelso,_Scottish_Borders" title="Kelso, Scottish Borders">Kelso</a>, attending <a href="/wiki/Kelso_High_School_(Scotland)" class="mw-redirect" title="Kelso High School (Scotland)">the local grammar school</a> where he met <a href="/wiki/James_Ballantyne" title="James Ballantyne">James</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_Ballantyne_(publisher)" title="John Ballantyne (publisher)">John Ballantyne</a>, who later became his business partners and printed his books.<sup id="cite_ref-uni_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-uni-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Meeting_with_Blacklock_and_Burns">Meeting with Blacklock and Burns</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Walter_Scott&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Meeting with Blacklock and Burns">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Scott began studying classics at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Edinburgh" title="University of Edinburgh">University of Edinburgh</a> in November 1783, at the age of 12, a year or so younger than most of his fellow students. In March 1786 he began an apprenticeship in his father's office to become a <a href="/wiki/Writer_to_the_Signet" class="mw-redirect" title="Writer to the Signet">Writer to the Signet</a>. While at the university Scott had become a friend of <a href="/wiki/Adam_Ferguson_(British_Army_officer)" title="Adam Ferguson (British Army officer)">Adam Ferguson </a>, the son of Professor <a href="/wiki/Adam_Ferguson" title="Adam Ferguson">Adam Ferguson</a> who hosted literary salons. Scott met the blind poet <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Blacklock" title="Thomas Blacklock">Thomas Blacklock</a>, who lent him books and introduced him to <a href="/wiki/James_Macpherson" title="James Macpherson">James Macpherson</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Ossian" title="Ossian">Ossian</a> cycle of poems. During the winter of 1786–87 the 15-year-old Scott saw <a href="/wiki/Robert_Burns" title="Robert Burns">Robert Burns</a> at one of these salons, for what was to be their only meeting. When Burns noticed a print illustrating the poem "The Justice of the Peace" and asked who had written the poem, only Scott knew that it was by <a href="/wiki/John_Langhorne_(poet)" title="John Langhorne (poet)">John Langhorne</a>, and was thanked by Burns. Scott describes this event in his memoirs where he whispers the answer to his friend Adam who tells Burns<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Another version of the event is described in Literary Beginnings<sup id="cite_ref-lit_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lit-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> When it was decided that he would become a lawyer, he returned to the university to study law, first taking classes in Moral Philosophy and Universal History in 1789–90.<sup id="cite_ref-uni_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-uni-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>After completing his studies in law, he became a lawyer in Edinburgh. As a lawyer's clerk he made his first visit to the Scottish Highlands directing an eviction. He was admitted to the <a href="/wiki/Faculty_of_Advocates" title="Faculty of Advocates">Faculty of Advocates</a> in 1792. He had an unsuccessful love suit with Williamina Belsches of Fettercairn, who married Scott's friend <a href="/wiki/Forbes_baronets#Forbes,_later_Stuart-Forbes,_of_Monymusk_(1626)" title="Forbes baronets">Sir William Forbes, 7th Baronet</a>.
</p>
<h3><span id="Start_of_literary_career.2C_marriage_and_family"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Start_of_literary_career,_marriage_and_family">Start of literary career, marriage and family</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Walter_Scott&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Start of literary career, marriage and family">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Scott%27s_Minstrelsy_Of_The_Scottish_Border.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Scott%27s_Minstrelsy_Of_The_Scottish_Border.JPG/220px-Scott%27s_Minstrelsy_Of_The_Scottish_Border.JPG" width="220" height="161" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Scott%27s_Minstrelsy_Of_The_Scottish_Border.JPG/330px-Scott%27s_Minstrelsy_Of_The_Scottish_Border.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Scott%27s_Minstrelsy_Of_The_Scottish_Border.JPG/440px-Scott%27s_Minstrelsy_Of_The_Scottish_Border.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2521" data-file-height="1848" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Scott%27s_Minstrelsy_Of_The_Scottish_Border.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A copy of Scott's <i>Minstrelsy</i> in the National Museum of Scotland</div></div></div>
<p>As a boy, youth and young man, Scott was fascinated by the oral traditions of the Scottish Borders. He was an obsessive collector of stories, and developed an innovative method of recording what he heard at the feet of local story-tellers using carvings on twigs, to avoid the disapproval of those who believed that such stories were neither for writing down nor for printing.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> At the age of 25 he began to write professionally, translating works from German,<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> his first publication being rhymed versions of ballads by <a href="/wiki/Gottfried_August_B%C3%BCrger" title="Gottfried August Bürger">Gottfried August Bürger</a> in 1796. He then published an idiosyncratic three-volume set of collected ballads of his adopted home region, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Minstrelsy_of_the_Scottish_Border" title="The Minstrelsy of the Scottish Border">The Minstrelsy of the Scottish Border</a></i>. This was the first sign from a literary standpoint of his interest in Scottish history.
</p><p>As a result of his early polio infection, Scott had a pronounced limp. He was described in 1820 as tall, well formed (except for one ankle and foot which made him walk lamely), neither fat nor thin, with forehead very high, nose short, upper lip long and face rather fleshy, complexion fresh and clear, eyes very blue, shrewd and penetrating, with hair now silvery white.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Although a determined walker, on horseback he experienced greater freedom of movement. Unable to consider a military career, Scott enlisted as a volunteer in the 1st Lothian and Border <a href="/wiki/Yeomanry" title="Yeomanry">yeomanry</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On a trip to the <a href="/wiki/Lake_District" title="Lake District">Lake District</a> with old college friends he met Charlotte Charpentier (or Carpenter), daughter of Jean Charpentier of <a href="/wiki/Lyon" title="Lyon">Lyon</a> in France, and ward of Lord Downshire in <a href="/wiki/Cumberland" title="Cumberland">Cumberland</a>, an Episcopalian. After three weeks of courtship, Scott proposed and they were married on Christmas Eve 1797 in St Mary's Church, Carlisle (a church set up in the now destroyed nave of <a href="/wiki/Carlisle_Cathedral" title="Carlisle Cathedral">Carlisle Cathedral</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> After renting a house in <a href="/wiki/George_Street,_Edinburgh" title="George Street, Edinburgh">George Street</a>, they moved to nearby South Castle Street. They had five children, of whom four survived by the time of Scott's death, most baptized by an Episcopalian clergyman. In 1799 he was appointed <a href="/wiki/Sheriff_Court" class="mw-redirect" title="Sheriff Court">Sheriff-Depute</a> of the <a href="/wiki/County_of_Selkirk" class="mw-redirect" title="County of Selkirk">County of Selkirk</a>, based in the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Burgh" class="mw-redirect" title="Royal Burgh">Royal Burgh</a> of <a href="/wiki/Selkirk,_Scottish_Borders" title="Selkirk, Scottish Borders">Selkirk</a>. In his early married days Scott had a decent living from his earnings at the law, his salary as Sheriff-Depute, his wife's income, some revenue from his writing, and his share of his father's rather meagre estate.
</p><p>After their third son was born in 1801, they moved to a spacious three-storey house built for Scott at 39 North Castle Street. This remained Scott's base in Edinburgh until 1826, when he could no longer afford two homes. From 1798 Scott had spent the summers in a cottage at <a href="/wiki/Lasswade" title="Lasswade">Lasswade</a>, where he entertained guests including literary figures, and it was there that his career as an author began. There were nominal residency requirements for his position of Sheriff-Depute, and at first he stayed at a local inn during the circuit. In 1804 he ended his use of the Lasswade cottage and leased the substantial house of <a href="/wiki/Ashiestiel" title="Ashiestiel">Ashestiel</a>, 6 miles (9.7&#160;km) from Selkirk.  It was sited on the south bank of the <a href="/wiki/River_Tweed" title="River Tweed">River Tweed</a>, and the building incorporated an old <a href="/wiki/Tower_house" title="Tower house">tower house</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-homes_6-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-homes-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Scott's father, also Walter (1729–1799), was a Freemason, being a member of Lodge St David, No.36 (Edinburgh), and Scott also became a Freemason in his father's Lodge in 1801, albeit only after the death of his father.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Poetry">Poetry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Walter_Scott&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Poetry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sir_William_Allan_-_Sir_Walter_Scott,_1771_-_1832._Novelist_and_poet_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Sir_William_Allan_-_Sir_Walter_Scott%2C_1771_-_1832._Novelist_and_poet_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/220px-Sir_William_Allan_-_Sir_Walter_Scott%2C_1771_-_1832._Novelist_and_poet_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" width="220" height="289" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Sir_William_Allan_-_Sir_Walter_Scott%2C_1771_-_1832._Novelist_and_poet_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/330px-Sir_William_Allan_-_Sir_Walter_Scott%2C_1771_-_1832._Novelist_and_poet_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Sir_William_Allan_-_Sir_Walter_Scott%2C_1771_-_1832._Novelist_and_poet_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/440px-Sir_William_Allan_-_Sir_Walter_Scott%2C_1771_-_1832._Novelist_and_poet_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3041" data-file-height="4001" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sir_William_Allan_-_Sir_Walter_Scott,_1771_-_1832._Novelist_and_poet_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sir Walter Scott, novelist and poet – painted by <a href="/wiki/Sir_William_Allan" class="mw-redirect" title="Sir William Allan">Sir William Allan</a></div></div></div>
<p>In 1796, Scott's friend <a href="/wiki/James_Ballantyne" title="James Ballantyne">James Ballantyne</a><sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> founded a printing press in Kelso, in the Scottish Borders. Through Ballantyne, Scott was able to publish his first works, including "<a href="/wiki/Glenfinlas_(poem)" title="Glenfinlas (poem)">Glenfinlas</a>" and "The Eve of St. John", and his poetry then began to bring him to public attention. In 1805, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Lay_of_the_Last_Minstrel" title="The Lay of the Last Minstrel">The Lay of the Last Minstrel</a></i> captured wide public imagination, and his career as a writer was established in spectacular fashion.
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><div class="poem">
<p>The way was long, the wind was cold,<br />
The Minstrel was infirm and old
</p>
</div><div class="templatequotecite">—&#8201;<cite>The Lay of the Last Minstrel (first lines)</cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>He published many other poems over the next ten years, including the popular <i><a href="/wiki/The_Lady_of_the_Lake_(poem)" title="The Lady of the Lake (poem)">The Lady of the Lake</a></i>, printed in 1810 and set in the <a href="/wiki/Trossachs" title="Trossachs">Trossachs</a>. Portions of the German translation of this work were set to music by <a href="/wiki/Franz_Schubert" title="Franz Schubert">Franz Schubert</a>. One of these songs, "<a href="/wiki/Ellens_dritter_Gesang" class="mw-redirect" title="Ellens dritter Gesang">Ellens dritter Gesang</a>", is popularly labelled as "Schubert's <i>Ave Maria</i>".
</p><p>Beethoven's opus 108 "<a href="/wiki/Twenty-Five_Scottish_Songs_(Beethoven)" title="Twenty-Five Scottish Songs (Beethoven)">Twenty-Five Scottish Songs</a>" includes 3 folk songs whose words are by Walter Scott.
</p><p><i><a href="/wiki/Marmion_(poem)" title="Marmion (poem)">Marmion</a></i>, published in 1808, produced lines that have become proverbial. Canto VI. Stanza 17 reads:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><div class="poem">
<p>Yet Clare's sharp questions must I shun<br />
Must separate Constance from the nun<br />
Oh! what a tangled web we weave<br />
When first we practise to deceive!<br />
A Palmer too! No wonder why<br />
I felt rebuked beneath his eye.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
</div>
</blockquote>
<p>In 1809 Scott persuaded James Ballantyne and his brother to move to Edinburgh and to establish their printing press there. He became a partner in their business. As a political conservative,<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> Scott helped to found the <a href="/wiki/Tory" title="Tory">Tory</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Quarterly_Review" title="Quarterly Review">Quarterly Review</a></i>, a review journal to which he made several anonymous contributions.  Scott was also a contributor to the <i><a href="/wiki/Edinburgh_Review" title="Edinburgh Review">Edinburgh Review</a></i>, which espoused <a href="/wiki/Whig_(British_political_party)" class="mw-redirect" title="Whig (British political party)">Whig</a> views.
</p><p>Scott was ordained as an <a href="/wiki/Ministers_and_elders_of_the_Church_of_Scotland" title="Ministers and elders of the Church of Scotland">elder</a> in the Presbyterian Church of Duddington and sat in the <a href="/wiki/General_Assembly_of_the_Church_of_Scotland" title="General Assembly of the Church of Scotland">General Assembly</a> for a time as representative elder of the burgh of Selkirk.
</p><p>When the lease of Ashestiel expired in 1811, Scott bought Cartley Hole Farm, on the south bank of the River Tweed nearer <a href="/wiki/Melrose,_Scottish_Borders" title="Melrose, Scottish Borders">Melrose</a>. The farm had the nickname of "<a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/clarty" class="extiw" title="wikt:clarty">Clarty</a> Hole" (Scots for "muddy hole"), and when Scott built a family cottage there in 1812 he named it "Abbotsford". He continued to expand the estate, and built <a href="/wiki/Abbotsford_House" title="Abbotsford House">Abbotsford House</a> in a series of extensions.<sup id="cite_ref-homes_6-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-homes-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1813 Scott was offered the position of <a href="/wiki/Poet_Laureate_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Poet Laureate of the United Kingdom">Poet Laureate</a>. He declined, due to concerns that "such an appointment would be a poisoned chalice", as the Laureateship had fallen into disrepute, due to the decline in quality of work suffered by previous title holders, "as a succession of poetasters had churned out conventional and obsequious odes on royal occasions."<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> He sought advice from the <a href="/wiki/Duke_of_Buccleuch" title="Duke of Buccleuch">Duke of Buccleuch</a>, who counseled him to retain his literary independence, and the position went to Scott's friend, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Southey" title="Robert Southey">Robert Southey</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Novels">Novels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Walter_Scott&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Novels">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Walter_Scott_Legend_of_Montrose_Illustration.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Walter_Scott_Legend_of_Montrose_Illustration.jpg/170px-Walter_Scott_Legend_of_Montrose_Illustration.jpg" width="170" height="235" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Walter_Scott_Legend_of_Montrose_Illustration.jpg/255px-Walter_Scott_Legend_of_Montrose_Illustration.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Walter_Scott_Legend_of_Montrose_Illustration.jpg/340px-Walter_Scott_Legend_of_Montrose_Illustration.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1726" data-file-height="2389" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Walter_Scott_Legend_of_Montrose_Illustration.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/A_Legend_of_Montrose" title="A Legend of Montrose">A Legend of Montrose</a></i>, illustration from the 1872 edition</div></div></div>
<p>Although Scott had attained worldwide celebrity through his poetry, he soon tried his hand at documenting his researches into the oral tradition of the Scottish Borders in prose fiction—stories and novels—at the time still considered aesthetically inferior to poetry (above all to such classical <a href="/wiki/Genre" title="Genre">genres</a> as the epic or poetic tragedy) as a <a href="/wiki/Mimesis" title="Mimesis">mimetic</a> vehicle for portraying historical events. In an innovative and astute action, he wrote and published his <a href="/wiki/Debut_novel" title="Debut novel">first novel</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Waverley_(novel)" title="Waverley (novel)">Waverley</a></i>, anonymously in 1814. It was a tale of the <a href="/wiki/Jacobite_rising_of_1745" title="Jacobite rising of 1745">Jacobite rising of 1745</a>. Its English protagonist, Edward Waverley, like <a href="/wiki/Don_Quixote" title="Don Quixote">Don Quixote</a> a great reader of romances, has been brought up by his Tory uncle, who is sympathetic to <a href="/wiki/Jacobitism" title="Jacobitism">Jacobitism</a>, although Edward's own father is a Whig. The youthful Waverley obtains a commission in the Whig army and is posted in <a href="/wiki/Dundee" title="Dundee">Dundee</a>. On leave, he meets his uncle's friend, the Jacobite Baron Bradwardine and is attracted to the Baron's daughter Rose. On a visit to the Highlands, Edward overstays his leave and is arrested and charged with desertion but is rescued by the Highland chieftain Fergus MacIvor and his mesmerizing sister Flora, whose devotion to the Stuart cause, "as it exceeded her brother's in fanaticism, excelled it also in purity".  Through Flora, Waverley meets <a href="/wiki/Charles_Edward_Stuart" title="Charles Edward Stuart">Bonnie Prince Charlie</a>, and under her influence goes over to the Jacobite side and takes part in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Prestonpans" title="Battle of Prestonpans">Battle of Prestonpans</a>. He escapes retribution, however, after saving the life of a Whig colonel during the battle. Waverley (whose surname reflects his divided loyalties) eventually decides to lead a peaceful life of establishment respectability under the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Hanover" title="House of Hanover">House of Hanover</a> rather than live as a proscribed rebel. He chooses to marry the beautiful Rose Bradwardine, rather than cast his lot with the <a href="/wiki/Sublime_(philosophy)" title="Sublime (philosophy)">sublime</a> Flora MacIvor, who, after the failure of the '45 rising, retires to a French convent.
</p><p>There followed a succession of novels over the next five years, each with a Scottish historical setting. Mindful of his reputation as a poet, Scott maintained the anonymity he had begun with <i>Waverley</i>,  publishing the novels under the name "Author of Waverley" or as "Tales of..." with no author. Among those familiar with his poetry, his identity became an open secret, but Scott persisted in maintaining the façade, perhaps because he thought his old-fashioned father would disapprove of his engaging in such a trivial pursuit as novel writing. During this time Scott became known by the nickname "The Wizard of the North". In 1815 he was given the honour of dining with <a href="/wiki/George_IV_of_Great_Britain" class="mw-redirect" title="George IV of Great Britain">George, Prince Regent</a>, who wanted to meet the "Author of Waverley".
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Charles_Robert_Leslie_-_Sir_Walter_Scott_-_Ravenswood_and_Lucy_at_the_Mermaiden%27s_Well_-_Bride_of_Lammermoor.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Charles_Robert_Leslie_-_Sir_Walter_Scott_-_Ravenswood_and_Lucy_at_the_Mermaiden%27s_Well_-_Bride_of_Lammermoor.jpg/220px-Charles_Robert_Leslie_-_Sir_Walter_Scott_-_Ravenswood_and_Lucy_at_the_Mermaiden%27s_Well_-_Bride_of_Lammermoor.jpg" width="220" height="381" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Charles_Robert_Leslie_-_Sir_Walter_Scott_-_Ravenswood_and_Lucy_at_the_Mermaiden%27s_Well_-_Bride_of_Lammermoor.jpg/330px-Charles_Robert_Leslie_-_Sir_Walter_Scott_-_Ravenswood_and_Lucy_at_the_Mermaiden%27s_Well_-_Bride_of_Lammermoor.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Charles_Robert_Leslie_-_Sir_Walter_Scott_-_Ravenswood_and_Lucy_at_the_Mermaiden%27s_Well_-_Bride_of_Lammermoor.jpg/440px-Charles_Robert_Leslie_-_Sir_Walter_Scott_-_Ravenswood_and_Lucy_at_the_Mermaiden%27s_Well_-_Bride_of_Lammermoor.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4076" data-file-height="7068" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Charles_Robert_Leslie_-_Sir_Walter_Scott_-_Ravenswood_and_Lucy_at_the_Mermaiden%27s_Well_-_Bride_of_Lammermoor.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"Edgar and Lucie at Mermaiden's well" by Charles Robert Leslie (1886), after Sir Walter Scott's <i>Bride of Lammermoor</i>. Lucie is wearing a <a href="/wiki/Full_plaid" title="Full plaid">full plaid</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Scott's 1819 series <i><a href="/wiki/Tales_of_my_Landlord" class="mw-redirect" title="Tales of my Landlord">Tales of my Landlord</a></i> is sometimes considered a subset of the <i>Waverley</i> novels and was intended to illustrate aspects of Scottish regional life. Among the best known is <i><a href="/wiki/The_Bride_of_Lammermoor" title="The Bride of Lammermoor">The Bride of Lammermoor</a></i>, a fictionalized version of an actual incident in the history of the <a href="/wiki/James_Dalrymple,_1st_Viscount_of_Stair#Biography" title="James Dalrymple, 1st Viscount of Stair">Dalrymple</a> family that took place in the <a href="/wiki/Lammermuir_Hills" title="Lammermuir Hills">Lammermuir Hills</a> in 1669. In the novel, Lucie Ashton and the nobly born but now dispossessed and impoverished Edgar Ravenswood exchange vows. But the Ravenswoods and the wealthy Ashtons, who now own the former Ravenswood lands, are enemies, and Lucie's mother forces her daughter to break her engagement to Edgar and marry the wealthy Sir Arthur Bucklaw. Lucie falls into a depression and on their wedding night stabs the bridegroom, succumbs to insanity, and dies. In 1821, French Romantic painter <a href="/wiki/Eug%C3%A8ne_Delacroix" title="Eugène Delacroix">Eugène Delacroix</a> painted a portrait depicting himself as the melancholy, disinherited Edgar Ravenswood. The prolonged, climactic <a href="/wiki/Coloratura" title="Coloratura">coloratura</a> mad scene for Lucia in <a href="/wiki/Gaetano_Donizetti" title="Gaetano Donizetti">Donizetti</a>'s 1835 <a href="/wiki/Bel_canto" title="Bel canto">bel canto</a> opera <i><a href="/wiki/Lucia_di_Lammermoor" title="Lucia di Lammermoor">Lucia di Lammermoor</a></i> is based on what in the novel were just a few bland sentences.
</p><p><i><a href="/wiki/Tales_of_my_Landlord" class="mw-redirect" title="Tales of my Landlord">Tales of my Landlord</a> </i>includes the now highly regarded novel <i><a href="/wiki/Old_Mortality" title="Old Mortality">Old Mortality</a></i>, set in 1679–89 against the backdrop of the ferocious anti-Covenanting campaign of the Tory <a href="/wiki/John_Graham,_1st_Viscount_Dundee" title="John Graham, 1st Viscount Dundee">Graham of Claverhouse</a>, subsequently made Viscount Dundee (called "Bluidy Clavers" by his opponents but later dubbed "<a href="/wiki/Bonnie_Dundee" title="Bonnie Dundee">Bonnie Dundee</a>" by Scott). The Covenanters were <a href="/wiki/Presbyterianism" title="Presbyterianism">presbyterians</a> who had supported the <a href="/wiki/Restoration_(Scotland)" title="Restoration (Scotland)">Restoration of Charles II</a> on promises of a Presbyterian settlement, but he had instead reintroduced <a href="/wiki/Episcopal_polity" title="Episcopal polity">Episcopalian church government</a> with draconian penalties for Presbyterian worship. This led to the destitution of around 270 ministers who had refused to take an oath of allegiance and submit themselves to bishops, and who continued to conduct worship among a remnant of their flock in caves and other remote country spots. The relentless persecution of these conventicles and attempts to break them up by military force had led to open revolt. The story is told from the point of view of Henry Morton, a moderate Presbyterian, who is unwittingly drawn into the conflict and barely escapes summary execution. In writing <i>Old Mortality</i> Scott drew upon the knowledge he had acquired from his researches into ballads on the subject for <i><a href="/wiki/The_Minstrelsy_of_the_Scottish_Border" title="The Minstrelsy of the Scottish Border">The Minstrelsy of the Scottish Border</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> Scott's background as a lawyer also informed his perspective, for at the time of the novel, which takes place before the <a href="/wiki/Act_of_Union_of_1707" class="mw-redirect" title="Act of Union of 1707">Act of Union of 1707</a>, English law did not apply in Scotland, and afterwards Scotland has continued to have its own <a href="/wiki/Scots_law" title="Scots law">Scots law</a> as a hybrid legal system. A recent critic, who is a legal as well as a literary scholar, argues that <i>Old Mortality</i> not only reflects the dispute between Stuart's <a href="/wiki/Absolute_monarchy" title="Absolute monarchy">absolute monarchy</a> and the jurisdiction of the courts, but also invokes a foundational moment in British sovereignty, namely, the <a href="/wiki/Habeas_Corpus_Act_1679" title="Habeas Corpus Act 1679">Habeas Corpus Act</a> (also known as the <a href="/wiki/Habeas_corpus" title="Habeas corpus">Great Writ</a>), passed by the <a href="/wiki/English_Parliament" class="mw-redirect" title="English Parliament">English Parliament</a> in 1679.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> Oblique reference to the origin of <i>Habeas corpus</i> underlies Scott's next novel, <i><a href="/wiki/Ivanhoe" title="Ivanhoe">Ivanhoe</a></i>, set during the era of the creation of the <a href="/wiki/Magna_Carta" title="Magna Carta">Magna Carta</a>, which political conservatives like Walter Scott and <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Burke" title="Edmund Burke">Edmund Burke</a> regarded as rooted in immemorial British custom and precedent.
</p><p><i><a href="/wiki/Ivanhoe" title="Ivanhoe">Ivanhoe</a></i> (1819), set in 12th-century England, marked a move away from Scott's focus on the local history of Scotland. Based partly on <a href="/wiki/David_Hume" title="David Hume">Hume's</a> <i><a href="/wiki/The_History_of_England_(Hume)" title="The History of England (Hume)">History of England</a></i> and the ballad cycle of <a href="/wiki/Robin_Hood" title="Robin Hood">Robin Hood</a>, <i>Ivanhoe</i> was quickly translated into many languages and inspired countless imitations and theatrical adaptations. <i>Ivanhoe</i> depicts the cruel tyranny of the Norman overlords (<a href="/wiki/Norman_Yoke" class="mw-redirect" title="Norman Yoke">Norman Yoke</a>)  over the impoverished Saxon populace of England, with two of the main characters, Rowena and Locksley (<a href="/wiki/Robin_Hood" title="Robin Hood">Robin Hood</a>), representing the dispossessed Saxon aristocracy. When the protagonists are captured and imprisoned by a Norman baron, Scott interrupts the story to exclaim:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>It is grievous to think that those valiant barons, to whose stand against the crown the liberties of England were indebted for their existence, should themselves have been such dreadful oppressors, and capable of excesses contrary not only to the laws of England, but to those of nature and humanity. But, alas&#160;...fiction itself can hardly reach the dark reality of the horrors of the period. (Chapter 24.33)
</p></blockquote>
<p>The institution of the <a href="/wiki/Magna_Carta" title="Magna Carta">Magna Carta</a>, which happens outside the time frame of the story, is portrayed as a progressive (incremental) reform, but also as a step towards the recovery of a lost golden age of liberty endemic to England and the English system. Scott puts a derisive prophecy in the mouth of the jester Wamba:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><div class="poem">
<p>Norman saw on English oak.<br />
On English neck a Norman yoke;<br />
Norman spoon to English dish,<br />
And England ruled as Normans wish;<br />
Blithe world in England never will be more,<br />
Till England's rid of all the four. (<i>Ivanhoe</i>, Ch. xxvii)
</p>
</div>
</blockquote>
<p>Although on the surface an entertaining escapist romance, alert contemporary readers would have quickly recognised the political subtext of <i>Ivanhoe</i>, which appeared immediately after the English Parliament, fearful of French-style revolution in the aftermath of <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Waterloo" title="Battle of Waterloo">Waterloo</a>, had passed the <a href="/wiki/Habeas_Corpus_Suspension_Act_1817" title="Habeas Corpus Suspension Act 1817">Habeas Corpus Suspension acts of 1817 and 1818</a> and other extremely repressive measures, and when traditional English Charter rights versus revolutionary human rights was a topic of discussion.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><i>Ivanhoe</i> was also remarkable in its sympathetic portrayal of Jewish characters: Rebecca, considered by many critics the book's real heroine, does not in the end get to marry Ivanhoe, whom she loves, but Scott allows her to remain faithful to her own religion, rather than having her convert to Christianity. Likewise, her father, Isaac of York, a Jewish moneylender, is shown as a victim rather than a villain. In <i>Ivanhoe</i>, which is one of Scott's Waverley novels, religious and sectarian fanatics are the villains, while the <a href="/wiki/Eponym" title="Eponym">eponymous</a> hero is a bystander who must weigh the evidence and decide where to take a stand. Scott's positive portrayal of Judaism, which reflects his humanity and concern for religious toleration, also coincided with a contemporary movement for the <a href="/wiki/Emancipation_of_the_Jews_in_England" class="mw-redirect" title="Emancipation of the Jews in England">Emancipation of the Jews in England</a>.
</p>
<h3><span id="Recovery_of_the_Crown_Jewels.2C_baronetcy_and_ceremonial_pageantry"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Recovery_of_the_Crown_Jewels,_baronetcy_and_ceremonial_pageantry">Recovery of the Crown Jewels, baronetcy and ceremonial pageantry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Walter_Scott&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Recovery of the Crown Jewels, baronetcy and ceremonial pageantry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rediscovering_the_lost_%27Honours_of_Scotland%27.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Rediscovering_the_lost_%27Honours_of_Scotland%27.jpg/220px-Rediscovering_the_lost_%27Honours_of_Scotland%27.jpg" width="220" height="167" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Rediscovering_the_lost_%27Honours_of_Scotland%27.jpg/330px-Rediscovering_the_lost_%27Honours_of_Scotland%27.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Rediscovering_the_lost_%27Honours_of_Scotland%27.jpg/440px-Rediscovering_the_lost_%27Honours_of_Scotland%27.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2500" data-file-height="1896" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Rediscovering_the_lost_%27Honours_of_Scotland%27.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Rediscovering the 'lost' <a href="/wiki/Honours_of_Scotland" title="Honours of Scotland">Honours of Scotland</a> in 1818</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:George_IV_landing_at_Leith.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/George_IV_landing_at_Leith.JPG/220px-George_IV_landing_at_Leith.JPG" width="220" height="149" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/George_IV_landing_at_Leith.JPG/330px-George_IV_landing_at_Leith.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/George_IV_landing_at_Leith.JPG/440px-George_IV_landing_at_Leith.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1410" data-file-height="956" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:George_IV_landing_at_Leith.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/George_IV_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="George IV of the United Kingdom">George IV</a> landing at <a href="/wiki/Leith" title="Leith">Leith</a> in 1822</div></div></div>
<p>Scott's fame grew as his explorations and interpretations of Scottish history and society captured popular imagination. Impressed by this, the Prince Regent (the future <a href="/wiki/George_IV_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="George IV of the United Kingdom">George IV</a>) gave Scott permission in a Royal Warrant dated 28th October 1817<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> to conduct a search for the Crown Jewels ("<a href="/wiki/Honours_of_Scotland" title="Honours of Scotland">Honours of Scotland</a>"). During the years of the Protectorate under <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Cromwell" title="Oliver Cromwell">Cromwell</a> the Crown Jewels had been hidden away, but had subsequently been used to crown <a href="/wiki/Charles_II_of_England" title="Charles II of England">Charles II</a>. They were not used to crown subsequent monarchs, but were regularly taken to sittings of Parliament, to represent the absent monarch, until the <a href="/wiki/Act_of_Union_1707" class="mw-redirect" title="Act of Union 1707">Act of Union 1707</a>. Thereafter, the honours were stored in Edinburgh Castle, but the large locked box in which they were stored was not opened for more than 100 years, and stories circulated that they had been "lost" or removed. On 4th February 1818<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>, Scott and a small team of military men opened the box, and "unearthed" the honours from the Crown Room of <a href="/wiki/Edinburgh_Castle" title="Edinburgh Castle">Edinburgh Castle</a>. A grateful Prince Regent granted Scott the title of <a href="/wiki/Baronet" title="Baronet">baronet</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Walter_Scott_Chronology_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Walter_Scott_Chronology-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> and in March 1820 he received the baronetcy in London, becoming Sir Walter Scott, 1st Baronet.<sup id="cite_ref-March_1820_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-March_1820-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>After George's accession to the throne, the <a href="/wiki/City_council_of_Edinburgh" class="mw-redirect" title="City council of Edinburgh">city council of Edinburgh</a> invited Scott, at the King's behest, to stage-manage the 1822 <a href="/wiki/Visit_of_King_George_IV_to_Scotland" title="Visit of King George IV to Scotland">visit of King George IV to Scotland</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Walter_Scott_Chronology_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Walter_Scott_Chronology-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> With only three weeks for planning and execution, Scott created a spectacular and comprehensive pageant, designed not only to impress the King, but also in some way to heal the rifts that had destabilised Scots society. He used the event to contribute to the drawing of a line under an old world that pitched his homeland into regular bouts of bloody strife. He, along with his "production team", mounted what in modern days could be termed a <a href="/wiki/Public_relations" title="Public relations">PR</a> event, in which the King was dressed in <a href="/wiki/Tartan" title="Tartan">tartan</a>, and was greeted by his people, many of whom were also dressed in similar tartan ceremonial dress. This form of dress, proscribed after the 1745 rebellion against the English, became one of the seminal, potent and ubiquitous symbols of Scottish identity.<sup id="cite_ref-Walterscott.lib.ed.ac.uk_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Walterscott.lib.ed.ac.uk-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In his novel <i><a href="/wiki/Kenilworth_(novel)" title="Kenilworth (novel)">Kenilworth</a></i>, Elizabeth I is welcomed to the castle of that name by means of an elaborate pageant, the details of which Scott was well qualified to itemize.
</p><p>Much of Scott's autograph work shows an almost stream-of-consciousness approach to writing. He included little in the way of punctuation in his drafts, leaving such details to the printers to supply.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> He eventually acknowledged in 1827 that he was the author of the <a href="/wiki/Waverley_Novels" title="Waverley Novels">Waverley Novels</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Walterscott.lib.ed.ac.uk_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Walterscott.lib.ed.ac.uk-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Financial_problems_and_death">Financial problems and death</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Walter_Scott&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Financial problems and death">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Walter_Scott_grave_Dryburgh_Abbey_20140527.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Walter_Scott_grave_Dryburgh_Abbey_20140527.jpg/220px-Walter_Scott_grave_Dryburgh_Abbey_20140527.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Walter_Scott_grave_Dryburgh_Abbey_20140527.jpg/330px-Walter_Scott_grave_Dryburgh_Abbey_20140527.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Walter_Scott_grave_Dryburgh_Abbey_20140527.jpg/440px-Walter_Scott_grave_Dryburgh_Abbey_20140527.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2560" data-file-height="1920" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Walter_Scott_grave_Dryburgh_Abbey_20140527.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sir Walter Scott's grave at <a href="/wiki/Dryburgh_Abbey" title="Dryburgh Abbey">Dryburgh Abbey</a></div></div></div>
<p>In 1825 a UK-wide <a href="/wiki/Panic_of_1825" title="Panic of 1825">banking crisis</a> resulted in the collapse of the Ballantyne printing business, of which Scott was the only partner with a financial interest; the company's debts of <a href="/wiki/Pound_sterling" title="Pound sterling">£</a>130,000 (equivalent to £9,800,000&#32;in 2016) caused his very public ruin.<sup id="cite_ref-McKinstry2002_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McKinstry2002-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> Rather than declare himself <a href="/wiki/Bankruptcy" title="Bankruptcy">bankrupt</a>, or to accept any kind of financial support from his many supporters and admirers (including the king himself), he placed his house and income in a trust belonging to his creditors, and determined to write his way out of debt. He kept up his prodigious output of fiction, as well as producing a biography of <a href="/wiki/Napoleon_Bonaparte" class="mw-redirect" title="Napoleon Bonaparte">Napoleon Bonaparte</a>, until 1831. By then his health was failing, but he nevertheless undertook a grand tour of Europe, and was welcomed and celebrated wherever he went. He returned to Scotland and, in September 1832, during the epidemic in Scotland that year, died of <a href="/wiki/Typhus" title="Typhus">typhus</a><sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>  at Abbotsford, the home he had designed and had built, near Melrose in the Scottish Borders. (His wife, Lady Scott, had died in 1826 and was buried as an Episcopalian.) Two Presbyterian ministers and one Episcopalian officiated at his funeral.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> Scott died owing money, but his novels continued to sell, and the debts encumbering his estate were discharged shortly after his death.<sup id="cite_ref-McKinstry2002_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McKinstry2002-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Personal_life">Personal life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Walter_Scott&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Personal life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Scott married Charlotte Carpenter in St Mary's Church, Carlisle Cathedral on Christmas Eve 1797.
</p><p>Scott's eldest son, Lt Walter Scott, inherited his father's estate and possessions. He married Jane Jobson, only daughter of William Jobson of <a href="/wiki/Lochore" title="Lochore">Lochore</a> (died 1822) and his wife Rachel Stuart (died 1863), on 3 February 1825.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Scott, Sr.'s lawyer from at least 1814 was Hay Donaldson <a href="/wiki/Society_of_Writers_to_Her_Majesty%27s_Signet" title="Society of Writers to Her Majesty&#39;s Signet">WS</a> (died 1822), who was also agent to the Duke of Buccleuch. Scott was Donaldson's proposer when he was elected a Fellow of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society_of_Edinburgh" title="Royal Society of Edinburgh">Royal Society of Edinburgh</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Scott was raised a <a href="/wiki/Presbyterian" class="mw-redirect" title="Presbyterian">Presbyterian</a> but later also adhered to the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Episcopal_Church" title="Scottish Episcopal Church">Scottish Episcopal Church</a>. Many have suggested this demonstrates both his nationalistic and unionistic tendencies.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> He was ordained as an elder in the Presbyterian Church of Duddington and sat in the General Assembly for a time as representative elder of the burgh of Selkirk.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> However, he received an Episcopal funeral at his own insistence.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> His Christian beliefs were explained and developed upon in his Religious Discourses of 1828.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>His distant cousin was the poet <a href="/wiki/Randall_Swingler" title="Randall Swingler">Randall Swingler</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Abbotsford">Abbotsford</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Walter_Scott&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Abbotsford">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Abbotsford04.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Abbotsford04.jpg/220px-Abbotsford04.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Abbotsford04.jpg/330px-Abbotsford04.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Abbotsford04.jpg/440px-Abbotsford04.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2362" data-file-height="1772" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Abbotsford04.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Abbotsford_House" title="Abbotsford House">Abbotsford House</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Tomb_of_Sir_W._Scott,_in_Dryburgh_Abbey_by_Henry_Fox_Talbot.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/The_Tomb_of_Sir_W._Scott%2C_in_Dryburgh_Abbey_by_Henry_Fox_Talbot.jpg/200px-The_Tomb_of_Sir_W._Scott%2C_in_Dryburgh_Abbey_by_Henry_Fox_Talbot.jpg" width="200" height="188" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/The_Tomb_of_Sir_W._Scott%2C_in_Dryburgh_Abbey_by_Henry_Fox_Talbot.jpg/300px-The_Tomb_of_Sir_W._Scott%2C_in_Dryburgh_Abbey_by_Henry_Fox_Talbot.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/The_Tomb_of_Sir_W._Scott%2C_in_Dryburgh_Abbey_by_Henry_Fox_Talbot.jpg/400px-The_Tomb_of_Sir_W._Scott%2C_in_Dryburgh_Abbey_by_Henry_Fox_Talbot.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="752" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Tomb_of_Sir_W._Scott,_in_Dryburgh_Abbey_by_Henry_Fox_Talbot.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Tomb of Walter Scott, in Dryburgh Abbey by <a href="/wiki/Henry_Fox_Talbot" title="Henry Fox Talbot">Henry Fox Talbot</a>, 1844</div></div></div>
<p>When Scott was a boy, he sometimes travelled with his father from Selkirk to Melrose, where some of his novels are set. At a certain spot the old gentleman would stop the carriage and take his son to a stone on the site of the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Melrose" title="Battle of Melrose">Battle of Melrose</a> (1526).<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>During the summers from 1804, Scott made his home at the large house of Ashestiel, on the south bank of the River Tweed 6 miles (9.7&#160;km) north of Selkirk. When his lease on this property expired in 1811, Scott bought Cartley Hole Farm, downstream on the Tweed nearer Melrose. The farm had the nickname of "<a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/clarty" class="extiw" title="wikt:clarty">Clarty</a> Hole", and when Scott built a family cottage there in 1812 he named it "Abbotsford". He continued to expand the estate, and built Abbotsford House in a series of extensions.<sup id="cite_ref-homes_6-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-homes-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
The farmhouse developed into a wonderful home that has been likened to a fairy palace. Scott was a pioneer of the Scottish Baronial style of architecture, therefore Abbotsford is festooned with turrets and stepped gabling. Through windows enriched with the insignia of heraldry the sun shone on suits of armour, trophies of the chase, a library of more than 9,000 volumes, fine furniture, and still finer pictures. Panelling of oak and cedar and carved ceilings relieved by coats of arms in their correct colours added to the beauty of the house.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag needs to be fact-checked with the cited source(s). (July 2013)">verification needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>It is estimated that the building cost Scott more than £25,000 (equivalent to £1,900,000&#32;in 2016). More land was purchased until Scott owned nearly 1,000 acres (4.0&#160;km<sup>2</sup>). A Roman road with a ford near Melrose used in olden days by the abbots of Melrose suggested the name of Abbotsford. Scott was buried in <a href="/wiki/Dryburgh_Abbey" title="Dryburgh Abbey">Dryburgh Abbey</a>, where his wife had earlier been interred. Nearby is a large statue of <a href="/wiki/William_Wallace" title="William Wallace">William Wallace</a>, one of Scotland's many romanticised historical figures.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> Abbotsford later gave its name to the <a href="/wiki/Abbotsford_Club" title="Abbotsford Club">Abbotsford Club</a>, founded in 1834 in memory of Sir Walter Scott.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Walter_Scott&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Legacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
