<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Later_assessment">Later assessment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Walter_Scott&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Later assessment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Walter_Scott_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_18396.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Walter Scott - Project Gutenberg eText 18396.jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Walter_Scott_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_18396.jpg/170px-Walter_Scott_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_18396.jpg" width="170" height="215" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Walter_Scott_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_18396.jpg/255px-Walter_Scott_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_18396.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Walter_Scott_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_18396.jpg/340px-Walter_Scott_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_18396.jpg 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="760" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Walter_Scott_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_18396.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div></div></div></div>
<p>Although he continued to be extremely popular and widely read, both at home and abroad,<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> Scott's critical reputation declined in the last half of the 19th century as serious writers turned from romanticism to realism, and Scott began to be regarded as an author suitable for children. This trend accelerated in the 20th century. For example, in his classic study  <i><a href="/wiki/Aspects_of_the_Novel" title="Aspects of the Novel">Aspects of the Novel</a></i> (1927), <a href="/wiki/E._M._Forster" title="E. M. Forster">E. M. Forster</a> harshly criticized Scott's clumsy and slapdash writing style, "flat" characters, and thin plots. In contrast, the novels of Scott's contemporary <a href="/wiki/Jane_Austen" title="Jane Austen">Jane Austen</a>, once appreciated only by the discerning few (including, as it happened, Scott himself) rose steadily in critical esteem, though Austen, as a female writer, was still faulted for her narrow ("feminine") choice of subject matter, which, unlike Scott, avoided the grand historical themes traditionally viewed as masculine.
</p><p>Nevertheless, Scott's importance as an innovator continued to be recognized. He was acclaimed as the inventor of the genre of the modern <a href="/wiki/Historical_novel" class="mw-redirect" title="Historical novel">historical novel</a> (which others trace to <a href="/wiki/Jane_Porter" title="Jane Porter">Jane Porter</a>, whose work in the genre predates Scott's) and the inspiration for enormous numbers of imitators and genre writers both in Britain and on the European continent. In the cultural sphere, Scott's Waverley novels played a significant part in the movement (begun with <a href="/wiki/James_Macpherson" title="James Macpherson">James Macpherson</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Ossian" title="Ossian">Ossian</a></i> cycle) in rehabilitating the public perception of the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Highlands" title="Scottish Highlands">Scottish Highlands</a> and its culture, which had been formerly suppressed as barbaric, and viewed in the southern mind as a breeding ground of hill bandits, religious fanaticism, and <a href="/wiki/Jacobitism" title="Jacobitism">Jacobite rebellions</a>. Scott served as chairman of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society_of_Edinburgh" title="Royal Society of Edinburgh">Royal Society of Edinburgh</a> and was also a member of the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Royal_Celtic_Society&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Royal Celtic Society (page does not exist)">Royal Celtic Society</a>. His own contribution to the reinvention of Scottish culture was enormous, even though his re-creations of the customs of the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Highlands" title="Scottish Highlands">Highlands</a> were fanciful at times, despite his extensive travels around his native country. It is a testament to Scott's contribution in creating a unified identity for Scotland that Edinburgh's central railway station, opened in 1854 by the <a href="/wiki/North_British_Railway" title="North British Railway">North British Railway</a>, is called <a href="/wiki/Edinburgh_Waverley_railway_station" title="Edinburgh Waverley railway station">Waverley</a>. The fact that Scott was a <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Lowlands" title="Scottish Lowlands">Lowland</a> <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Scotland" title="Church of Scotland">Presbyterian</a>, rather than a Gaelic-speaking Catholic Highlander, made him more acceptable to a conservative English reading public. Scott's novels were certainly influential in the making of the Victorian craze for all things Scottish among British royalty, who were anxious to claim legitimacy through their rather attenuated historical connection with the royal house of Stuart.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>At the time Scott wrote, Scotland was poised to move away from an era of socially divisive clan warfare to a modern world of literacy and industrial capitalism. Through the medium of Scott's novels, the violent religious and political conflicts of the country's recent past could be seen as belonging to history—which Scott defined, as the subtitle of <i>Waverley</i> ("'Tis Sixty Years Since") indicates, as something that happened at least 60 years ago. Scott's advocacy of objectivity and moderation and his strong repudiation of political violence on either side also had a strong, though unspoken, contemporary resonance in an era when many conservative English speakers lived in mortal fear of a revolution in the French style on British soil. Scott's orchestration of <a href="/wiki/Visit_of_King_George_IV_to_Scotland" title="Visit of King George IV to Scotland">King George IV's visit to Scotland</a>, in 1822, was a pivotal event intended to inspire a view of his home country that, in his view, accentuated the positive aspects of the past while allowing the age of quasi-medieval blood-letting to be put to rest, while envisioning a more useful, peaceful future.
</p><p>After Scott's work had been essentially unstudied for many decades, a revival of critical interest began from the 1960s. <a href="/wiki/Postmodern" class="mw-redirect" title="Postmodern">Postmodern</a> tastes favoured discontinuous narratives and the introduction of the "first person", yet they were more favourable to Scott's work than Modernist tastes. While <a href="/wiki/F._R._Leavis" title="F. R. Leavis">F. R. Leavis</a> had disdained Scott, seeing him as a thoroughly bad novelist and a thoroughly bad influence (<i><a href="/wiki/The_Great_Tradition" title="The Great Tradition">The Great Tradition</a></i> [1948]), <a href="/wiki/Gy%C3%B6rgy_Luk%C3%A1cs" title="György Lukács">György Lukács</a> (<i>The Historical Novel</i> [1937, trans. 1962]) and <a href="/wiki/David_Daiches" title="David Daiches">David Daiches</a> (<i>Scott's Achievement as a Novelist</i> [1951]) offered a Marxian political reading of Scott's fiction that generated a great deal of genuine interest in his work. Scott is now seen as an important innovator and a key figure in the development of Scottish and world literature, and particularly as the principal inventor of the historical novel.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Memorials_and_commemoration">Memorials and commemoration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Walter_Scott&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Memorials and commemoration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Scott_Monument_from_St._David_Street.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Scott_Monument_from_St._David_Street.jpg/170px-Scott_Monument_from_St._David_Street.jpg" width="170" height="224" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Scott_Monument_from_St._David_Street.jpg/255px-Scott_Monument_from_St._David_Street.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Scott_Monument_from_St._David_Street.jpg/340px-Scott_Monument_from_St._David_Street.jpg 2x" data-file-width="485" data-file-height="640" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Scott_Monument_from_St._David_Street.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Scott_Monument" title="Scott Monument">Scott Monument</a> on Edinburgh's <a href="/wiki/Princes_Street" title="Princes Street">Princes Street</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sir_Walter_Scott_statue_at_Scott_Monument.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Sir_Walter_Scott_statue_at_Scott_Monument.jpg/170px-Sir_Walter_Scott_statue_at_Scott_Monument.jpg" width="170" height="255" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Sir_Walter_Scott_statue_at_Scott_Monument.jpg/255px-Sir_Walter_Scott_statue_at_Scott_Monument.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Sir_Walter_Scott_statue_at_Scott_Monument.jpg/340px-Sir_Walter_Scott_statue_at_Scott_Monument.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3029" data-file-height="4544" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sir_Walter_Scott_statue_at_Scott_Monument.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Statue by <a href="/wiki/John_Steell" title="John Steell">Sir John Steell</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Scott_Monument" title="Scott Monument">Scott Monument</a> in Edinburgh</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Scott_Monument,_Glasgow.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Scott_Monument%2C_Glasgow.JPG/170px-Scott_Monument%2C_Glasgow.JPG" width="170" height="233" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Scott_Monument%2C_Glasgow.JPG/255px-Scott_Monument%2C_Glasgow.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Scott_Monument%2C_Glasgow.JPG/340px-Scott_Monument%2C_Glasgow.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1856" data-file-height="2549" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Scott_Monument,_Glasgow.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Scott Monument in Glasgow's <a href="/wiki/George_Square" title="George Square">George Square</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Walter_Scott_statue,_Glasgow.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Walter_Scott_statue%2C_Glasgow.JPG/170px-Walter_Scott_statue%2C_Glasgow.JPG" width="170" height="253" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Walter_Scott_statue%2C_Glasgow.JPG/255px-Walter_Scott_statue%2C_Glasgow.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Walter_Scott_statue%2C_Glasgow.JPG/340px-Walter_Scott_statue%2C_Glasgow.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1721" data-file-height="2557" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Walter_Scott_statue,_Glasgow.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Statue on the Glasgow monument</div></div></div>
<p>During his lifetime, Scott's portrait was painted by Sir <a href="/wiki/Edwin_Landseer" title="Edwin Landseer">Edwin Landseer</a> and fellow Scots Sir <a href="/wiki/Henry_Raeburn" title="Henry Raeburn">Henry Raeburn</a> and <a href="/wiki/James_Eckford_Lauder" title="James Eckford Lauder">James Eckford Lauder</a>. In Edinburgh, the 61.1-metre-tall <a href="/wiki/Victorian_Gothic" class="mw-redirect" title="Victorian Gothic">Victorian Gothic</a> spire of the <a href="/wiki/Scott_Monument" title="Scott Monument">Scott Monument</a> was designed by <a href="/wiki/George_Meikle_Kemp" title="George Meikle Kemp">George Meikle Kemp</a>. It was completed in 1844, 12 years after Scott's death, and dominates the south side of <a href="/wiki/Princes_Street" title="Princes Street">Princes Street</a>. Scott is also commemorated on a stone slab in <a href="/wiki/Makars%27_Court" title="Makars&#39; Court">Makars' Court</a>, outside The Writers' Museum, <a href="/wiki/Lawnmarket" class="mw-redirect" title="Lawnmarket">Lawnmarket</a>, Edinburgh, along with other prominent Scottish writers; quotes from his work are also visible on the <a href="/wiki/Canongate" class="mw-redirect" title="Canongate">Canongate</a> Wall of the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Parliament_building" class="mw-redirect" title="Scottish Parliament building">Scottish Parliament building</a> in <a href="/wiki/Holyrood,_Edinburgh" title="Holyrood, Edinburgh">Holyrood</a>. There is a tower dedicated to his memory on <a href="/wiki/Corstorphine_Hill" title="Corstorphine Hill">Corstorphine Hill</a> in the west of the city and, as mentioned, Edinburgh's Waverley railway station takes its name from one of his novels.
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Glasgow" title="Glasgow">Glasgow</a>, <a href="/wiki/Public_statues_in_Glasgow#George_Square" class="mw-redirect" title="Public statues in Glasgow">Walter Scott's Monument</a> dominates the centre of <a href="/wiki/George_Square" title="George Square">George Square</a>, the main public square in the city. Designed by <a href="/wiki/David_Rhind" title="David Rhind">David Rhind</a> in 1838, the monument features a large column topped by a statue of Scott.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>
There is a statue of Scott in New York City's <a href="/wiki/Central_Park" title="Central Park">Central Park</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Numerous Masonic Lodges have been named after him and his novels. For example: Lodge Sir Walter Scott, No. 859 (Perth, Australia) and Lodge Waverley, No. 597 (Edinburgh, Scotland).<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The annual <a href="/wiki/Walter_Scott_Prize_for_Historical_Fiction" class="mw-redirect" title="Walter Scott Prize for Historical Fiction">Walter Scott Prize for Historical Fiction</a> was created in 2010 by the <a href="/wiki/Duke_of_Buccleuch" title="Duke of Buccleuch">Duke and Duchess of Buccleuch</a>, whose ancestors were closely linked to Sir Walter Scott. At £25,000, it is one of the largest prizes in British literature. The award has been presented at Scott's historic home, Abbotsford House.
</p><p>Scott has been credited with rescuing the <a href="/wiki/Banknotes_of_the_pound_sterling" title="Banknotes of the pound sterling">Scottish banknote</a>. In 1826, there was outrage in Scotland at the attempt of <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Parliament of the United Kingdom">Parliament</a> to prevent the production of banknotes of less than five pounds. Scott wrote a series of letters to the <i>Edinburgh Weekly Journal</i> under the pseudonym "<i><a href="/wiki/Malachi_Malagrowther" class="mw-redirect" title="Malachi Malagrowther">Malachi Malagrowther</a></i>" for retaining the right of Scottish banks to issue their own banknotes. This provoked such a response that the Government was forced to relent and allow the Scottish banks to continue printing pound notes. This campaign is commemorated by his continued appearance on the front of all notes issued by the <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_Scotland" title="Bank of Scotland">Bank of Scotland</a>. The image on the 2007 series of banknotes is based on the portrait by <a href="/wiki/Henry_Raeburn" title="Henry Raeburn">Henry Raeburn</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>During and immediately after World War I there was a movement spearheaded by <a href="/wiki/Woodrow_Wilson" title="Woodrow Wilson">President Wilson</a> and other eminent people to <a href="/wiki/Effect_of_World_War_I_on_children_in_the_United_States" title="Effect of World War I on children in the United States">inculcate patriotism in American school children</a>, especially immigrants, and to stress the American connection with the literature and institutions of the "mother country" of Great Britain, using selected readings in middle school textbooks.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> Scott's <i>Ivanhoe</i> continued to be required reading for many American high school students until the end of the 1950s.
</p><p>A bust of Scott is in the Hall of Heroes of the <a href="/wiki/National_Wallace_Monument" class="mw-redirect" title="National Wallace Monument">National Wallace Monument</a> in <a href="/wiki/Stirling" title="Stirling">Stirling</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Literature_by_other_authors">Literature by other authors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Walter_Scott&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Literature by other authors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table role="presentation" class="mbox-small plainlinks sistersitebox" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000">
<tbody><tr>
<td class="mbox-image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/38px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png" width="38" height="40" class="noviewer" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/57px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/76px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="410" data-file-height="430" /></td>
<td class="mbox-text plainlist"><a href="/wiki/Wikisource" title="Wikisource">Wikisource</a> has original text related to this article:
<div style="margin-left: 10px;"><b><a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Landon_in_The_Literary_Gazette_1832/On_Walter_Scott" class="extiw" title="wikisource:Landon in The Literary Gazette 1832/On Walter Scott">'On Walter Scott',<br /> a poem by L. E. L.</a></b></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<table role="presentation" class="mbox-small plainlinks sistersitebox" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000">
<tbody><tr>
<td class="mbox-image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/38px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png" width="38" height="40" class="noviewer" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/57px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/76px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="410" data-file-height="430" /></td>
<td class="mbox-text plainlist"><a href="/wiki/Wikisource" title="Wikisource">Wikisource</a> has original text related to this article:
<div style="margin-left: 10px;"><b><a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Letitia_Elizabeth_Landon_(L._E._L.)_in_Fisher%27s_Drawing_Room_Scrap_Book,_1833/Walter_Scott" class="extiw" title="wikisource:Letitia Elizabeth Landon (L. E. L.) in Fisher&#39;s Drawing Room Scrap Book, 1833/Walter Scott">'Sir Walter Scott',<br />a poem by L. E. L.</a></b></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><a href="/wiki/Letitia_Elizabeth_Landon" title="Letitia Elizabeth Landon">Letitia Elizabeth Landon</a> was a great admirer of Scott and, on his death, she wrote two tributes to him: <i>On Walter Scott</i> in the Literary Gazette, and <i>Sir Walter Scott</i> in Fisher's Drawing Room Scrap Book, 1833. Towards the end of her life she began a series called <i>The Female Picture Gallery</i> with a series of character analyses based on the women in Scott's works.
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Charles_Baudelaire" title="Charles Baudelaire">Charles Baudelaire</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/La_Fanfarlo" title="La Fanfarlo">La Fanfarlo</a></i> (1847), poet Samuel Cramer says of Scott:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Oh that tedious author, a dusty exhumer of chronicles! A fastidious mass of descriptions of bric-a-brac&#160;... and castoff things of every sort, armor, tableware, furniture, gothic inns, and melodramatic castles where lifeless mannequins stalk about, dressed in leotards.
</p>
</blockquote>
<p>In the novella, however, Cramer proves as deluded a romantic as any hero in one of Scott's novels.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Anne_Bront%C3%AB" title="Anne Brontë">Anne Brontë</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Tenant_of_Wildfell_Hall" title="The Tenant of Wildfell Hall">The Tenant of Wildfell Hall</a></i> (1848) the narrator, Gilbert Markham, brings an elegantly bound copy of <i><a href="/wiki/Marmion_(poem)" title="Marmion (poem)">Marmion</a></i> as a present to the independent "tenant of Wildfell Hall" (Helen Graham) whom he is courting, and is mortified when she insists on paying for it.
</p><p>In a speech delivered at Salem, Massachusetts, on 6 January 1860, to raise money for the families of the executed abolitionist <a href="/wiki/John_Brown_(abolitionist)" title="John Brown (abolitionist)">John Brown</a> and his followers, <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Waldo_Emerson" title="Ralph Waldo Emerson">Ralph Waldo Emerson</a> calls Brown an example of true chivalry, which consists not in noble birth but in helping the weak and defenseless and declares that  "Walter Scott would have delighted to draw his picture and trace his adventurous career".<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In his 1870 memoir, <i>Army Life in a Black Regiment</i>, New England abolitionist <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Wentworth_Higginson" title="Thomas Wentworth Higginson">Thomas Wentworth Higginson</a> (later editor of <a href="/wiki/Emily_Dickinson" title="Emily Dickinson">Emily Dickinson</a>), described how he wrote down and preserved Negro spirituals or "shouts" while serving as a colonel in the <a href="/wiki/First_South_Carolina_Volunteers" class="mw-redirect" title="First South Carolina Volunteers">First South Carolina Volunteers</a>, the first authorized Union Army regiment recruited from freedmen during the Civil War (memorialized in the 1989 film <a href="/wiki/Glory_(1989_film)" title="Glory (1989 film)"><i>Glory</i></a>). He wrote that he was "a faithful student of the Scottish ballads, and had always envied Sir Walter the delight of tracing them out amid their own heather, and of writing them down piecemeal from the lips of aged crones".
</p><p>According to his daughter <a href="/wiki/Eleanor_Marx" title="Eleanor Marx">Eleanor</a>, Scott was "an author to whom <a href="/wiki/Karl_Marx" title="Karl Marx">Karl Marx</a> again and again returned, whom he admired and knew as well as he did Balzac and Fielding".<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In his 1883 <i><a href="/wiki/Life_on_the_Mississippi" title="Life on the Mississippi">Life on the Mississippi</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Mark_Twain" title="Mark Twain">Mark Twain</a> satirized the impact of Scott's writings, declaring (with humorous hyperbole) that Scott "had so large a hand in making Southern character, as it existed before the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War"> [American Civil] war</a>", that he is "in great measure responsible for the war".<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> He goes on to coin the term "Sir Walter Scott disease", which he blames for the South's lack of advancement. Twain also targeted Scott in <i><a href="/wiki/Adventures_of_Huckleberry_Finn" title="Adventures of Huckleberry Finn">Adventures of Huckleberry Finn</a></i>, where he names a sinking boat the "Walter Scott" (1884); and, in <i><a href="/wiki/A_Connecticut_Yankee_in_King_Arthur%27s_Court" title="A Connecticut Yankee in King Arthur&#39;s Court">A Connecticut Yankee in King Arthur's Court</a></i> (1889), the main character repeatedly utters "great Scott" as an oath; by the end of the book, however, he has become absorbed in the world of knights in armor, reflecting Twain's ambivalence on the topic.
</p><p>The idyllic Cape Cod retreat of suffragists Verena Tarrant and Olive Chancellor in Henry James' <i><a href="/wiki/The_Bostonians" title="The Bostonians">The Bostonians</a></i> (1886) is called Marmion, evoking what James considered the Quixotic idealism of these social reformers.
</p><p>In <i><a href="/wiki/To_the_Lighthouse" title="To the Lighthouse">To the Lighthouse</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Virginia_Woolf" title="Virginia Woolf">Virginia Woolf</a>, Mrs. Ramsey glances at her husband:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>He was reading something that moved him very much&#160;... He was tossing the pages over. He was acting it – perhaps he was thinking himself the person in the book. She wondered what book it was. Oh, it was one of old Sir Walter's she saw, adjusting the shade of her lamp so that the light fell on her knitting. For Charles Tansley had been saying (she looked up as if she expected to hear the crash of books on the floor above) – had been saying that people don’t read Scott any more. Then her husband thought, "That's what they’ll say of me;" so he went and got one of those books&#160;... It fortified him. He clean forgot all the little rubs and digs of the evening... and his being so irritable with his wife and so touchy and minding when they passed his books over as if they didn’t exist at all&#160;...[Scott's] feeling for straight forward simple things, these fishermen, the poor old crazed creature in <a href="/wiki/Saunders_Mucklebackit" title="Saunders Mucklebackit">Mucklebackit</a>'s cottage [in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Antiquary" title="The Antiquary">The Antiquary</a></i>] made him feel so vigorous, so relieved of something that he felt roused and triumphant and could not choke back his tears. Raising the book a little to hide his face he let them fall and shook his head from side to side and forgot himself completely (but not one or two reflections about morality and French novels and English novels and Scott's hands being tied but his view perhaps being as true as the other view), forgot his own bothers and failures completely in poor Steenie's drowning and Mucklebackit's sorrow (that was Scott at his best) and the astonishing delight and feeling of vigor that it gave him. 
</p><p>Well, let them improve upon that, he thought as he finished the chapter&#160;... The whole of life did not consist in going to bed with a woman, he thought, returning to Scott and Balzac, to the English novel and the French novel.
</p>
</blockquote>
<p>In 1951, science-fiction author <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Asimov" title="Isaac Asimov">Isaac Asimov</a> wrote <i><a href="/wiki/Breeds_There_a_Man...%3F" title="Breeds There a Man...?">Breeds There a Man...?</a></i>, a short story with a title alluding vividly to Scott's <i><a href="/wiki/The_Lay_of_the_Last_Minstrel" title="The Lay of the Last Minstrel">The Lay of the Last Minstrel</a></i> (1805).
</p><p>In <i><a href="/wiki/To_Kill_a_Mockingbird" title="To Kill a Mockingbird">To Kill a Mockingbird</a></i> (1960), the protagonist's brother is made to read Walter Scott's book <i><a href="/wiki/Ivanhoe" title="Ivanhoe">Ivanhoe</a></i> to the ailing Mrs. Henry Lafayette Dubose, and he refers to the author as "Sir Walter Scout", in reference to his own sister's nickname.
</p><p>In <i><a href="/wiki/Mother_Night" title="Mother Night">Mother Night</a></i> (1961) by <a href="/wiki/Kurt_Vonnegut" title="Kurt Vonnegut">Kurt Vonnegut</a> Jr., <a href="/wiki/Memoir" title="Memoir">memoirist</a> and playwright Howard W. Campbell Jr. prefaces his text with the six lines beginning "Breathes there the man..."
</p><p>In <i>Knights of the Sea</i> (2010) by Canadian author <a href="/wiki/Paul_Marlowe" title="Paul Marlowe">Paul Marlowe</a>, there are several quotes from and references to <i><a href="/wiki/Marmion_(poem)" title="Marmion (poem)">Marmion</a></i>, as well as an inn named after <i><a href="/wiki/Ivanhoe" title="Ivanhoe">Ivanhoe</a></i>, and a fictitious Scott novel entitled <i>The Beastmen of Glen Glammoch</i>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Bibliography">Bibliography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Walter_Scott&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Bibliography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sir_Walter_Scott,_Bart._of_Abbotsford_from_%22The_Scottish_Bar_Fifty_Years_Ago%22.PNG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Sir_Walter_Scott%2C_Bart._of_Abbotsford_from_%22The_Scottish_Bar_Fifty_Years_Ago%22.PNG/220px-Sir_Walter_Scott%2C_Bart._of_Abbotsford_from_%22The_Scottish_Bar_Fifty_Years_Ago%22.PNG" width="220" height="294" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/78/Sir_Walter_Scott%2C_Bart._of_Abbotsford_from_%22The_Scottish_Bar_Fifty_Years_Ago%22.PNG 1.5x" data-file-width="229" data-file-height="306" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sir_Walter_Scott,_Bart._of_Abbotsford_from_%22The_Scottish_Bar_Fifty_Years_Ago%22.PNG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sir Walter Scott by <a href="/wiki/Robert_Scott_Moncrieff" title="Robert Scott Moncrieff">Robert Scott Moncrieff</a>.</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Novels_2">Novels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Walter_Scott&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Novels">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Waverley_Novels" title="Waverley Novels">Waverley Novels</a> is the title given to the long series of Scott novels released from 1814 to 1832 which takes its name from the first novel, <i><a href="/wiki/Waverley_(novel)" title="Waverley (novel)">Waverley</a></i>. The following is a chronological list of the entire series:
</p>
<ul><li>1814: <i><a href="/wiki/Waverley_(novel)" title="Waverley (novel)">Waverley</a></i></li>
<li>1815: <i><a href="/wiki/Guy_Mannering" title="Guy Mannering">Guy Mannering</a></i></li>
<li>1816: <i><a href="/wiki/The_Antiquary" title="The Antiquary">The Antiquary</a></i></li>
<li>1816: <i><a href="/wiki/The_Black_Dwarf_(novel)" title="The Black Dwarf (novel)">The Black Dwarf</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Old_Mortality" title="Old Mortality">The Tale of Old Mortality</a></i> – the 1st installment from the subset series, <a href="/wiki/Tales_of_My_Landlord" title="Tales of My Landlord">Tales of My Landlord</a></li>
<li>1817: <i><a href="/wiki/Rob_Roy_(novel)" title="Rob Roy (novel)">Rob Roy</a></i></li>
<li>1818: <i><a href="/wiki/The_Heart_of_Midlothian" title="The Heart of Midlothian">The Heart of Midlothian</a></i> – the 2nd installment from the subset series, <a href="/wiki/Tales_of_My_Landlord" title="Tales of My Landlord">Tales of My Landlord</a></li>
<li>1819: <i><a href="/wiki/The_Bride_of_Lammermoor" title="The Bride of Lammermoor">The Bride of Lammermoor</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/A_Legend_of_Montrose" title="A Legend of Montrose">A Legend of Montrose</a></i> – the 3rd installment from the subset series, <a href="/wiki/Tales_of_My_Landlord" title="Tales of My Landlord">Tales of My Landlord</a></li>
<li>1820: <i><a href="/wiki/Ivanhoe" title="Ivanhoe">Ivanhoe</a></i></li>
<li>1820: <i><a href="/wiki/The_Monastery" title="The Monastery">The Monastery</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Abbot" title="The Abbot">The Abbot</a></i> – from the subset series, <a href="/wiki/Tales_from_Benedictine_Sources" title="Tales from Benedictine Sources">Tales from Benedictine Sources</a></li>
<li>1821: <i><a href="/wiki/Kenilworth_(novel)" title="Kenilworth (novel)">Kenilworth</a></i></li>
<li>1822: <i><a href="/wiki/The_Pirate_(novel)" title="The Pirate (novel)">The Pirate</a></i></li>
<li>1822: <i><a href="/wiki/The_Fortunes_of_Nigel" title="The Fortunes of Nigel">The Fortunes of Nigel</a></i></li>
<li>1822: <i><a href="/wiki/Peveril_of_the_Peak" title="Peveril of the Peak">Peveril of the Peak</a></i></li>
<li>1823: <i><a href="/wiki/Quentin_Durward" title="Quentin Durward">Quentin Durward</a></i></li>
<li>1824: <i><a href="/wiki/St._Ronan%27s_Well" class="mw-redirect" title="St. Ronan&#39;s Well">St. Ronan's Well</a></i></li>
<li>1824: <i><a href="/wiki/Redgauntlet" title="Redgauntlet">Redgauntlet</a></i></li>
<li>1825: <i><a href="/wiki/The_Betrothed_(Walter_Scott_novel)" class="mw-redirect" title="The Betrothed (Walter Scott novel)">The Betrothed</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Talisman_(Scott_novel)" title="The Talisman (Scott novel)">The Talisman</a></i> – from the subset series, <a href="/wiki/Tales_of_the_Crusaders" title="Tales of the Crusaders">Tales of the Crusaders</a></li>
<li>1826: <i><a href="/wiki/Woodstock_(novel)" title="Woodstock (novel)">Woodstock</a></i></li>
<li>1828: <i><a href="/wiki/The_Fair_Maid_of_Perth" title="The Fair Maid of Perth">The Fair Maid of Perth</a></i> – the 2nd installment from the subset series, <a href="/wiki/Chronicles_of_the_Canongate" title="Chronicles of the Canongate">Chronicles of the Canongate</a> (sometimes not considered as part of the <a href="/wiki/Waverley_Novels" title="Waverley Novels">Waverley Novels</a> series)</li>
<li>1829: <i><a href="/wiki/Anne_of_Geierstein" title="Anne of Geierstein">Anne of Geierstein</a></i></li>
<li>1832: <i><a href="/wiki/Count_Robert_of_Paris" title="Count Robert of Paris">Count Robert of Paris</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Castle_Dangerous" title="Castle Dangerous">Castle Dangerous</a></i> – the 4th installment from the subset series, <a href="/wiki/Tales_of_My_Landlord" title="Tales of My Landlord">Tales of My Landlord</a></li></ul>
<p>Other novels:
</p>
<ul><li>1831–1832: <i><a href="/wiki/The_Siege_of_Malta_(novel)" title="The Siege of Malta (novel)">The Siege of Malta</a></i> – a finished novel published posthumously in 2008</li>
<li>1832: <i><a href="/wiki/Bizarro_(novel)" title="Bizarro (novel)">Bizarro</a></i> – an unfinished novel (or novella) published posthumously in 2008</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Poetry_2">Poetry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Walter_Scott&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Poetry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Many of the short poems or songs released by Scott (or later anthologized) were originally not separate pieces but parts of longer poems interspersed throughout his novels, tales, and dramas.
</p>
<ul><li>1796: "The Chase" – an <a href="/wiki/English_(language)" class="mw-redirect" title="English (language)">English-language</a> translation of the <a href="/wiki/German_(language)" class="mw-redirect" title="German (language)">German-language</a> poem by <a href="/wiki/Gottfried_August_B%C3%BCrger" title="Gottfried August Bürger">Gottfried August Bürger</a> entitled "Der Wilde Jäger" (or, "The Wild Huntsmen", its more common English translation), first of the <a href="/wiki/Translations_and_Imitations_from_German_Ballads_by_Sir_Walter_Scott" title="Translations and Imitations from German Ballads by Sir Walter Scott">translations and imitations from German ballads by Scott</a></li>
<li>1802–1803: <i><a href="/wiki/The_Minstrelsy_of_the_Scottish_Border" title="The Minstrelsy of the Scottish Border">The Minstrelsy of the Scottish Border</a></i></li>
<li>1805: <i><a href="/wiki/The_Lay_of_the_Last_Minstrel" title="The Lay of the Last Minstrel">The Lay of the Last Minstrel</a></i></li>
<li>1806: <i>Ballads and Lyrical Pieces</i></li>
<li>1808: <a href="/wiki/Marmion_(poem)" title="Marmion (poem)"><i>Marmion</i></a></li>
<li>1810: <a href="/wiki/The_Lady_of_the_Lake_(poem)" title="The Lady of the Lake (poem)"><i>The Lady of the Lake</i></a></li>
<li>1811: <i><a href="/wiki/The_Vision_of_Don_Roderick" title="The Vision of Don Roderick">The Vision of Don Roderick</a></i></li>
<li>1813: <a href="/wiki/The_Bridal_of_Triermain_by_Walter_Scott" class="mw-redirect" title="The Bridal of Triermain by Walter Scott"><i>The Bridal of Triermain</i></a></li>
<li>1813: <a href="/wiki/Rokeby_(poem)" title="Rokeby (poem)"><i>Rokeby</i></a></li>
<li>1815: <i><a href="/wiki/The_Field_of_Waterloo" title="The Field of Waterloo">The Field of Waterloo</a></i></li>
<li>1815: <i><a href="/wiki/The_Lord_of_the_Isles" title="The Lord of the Isles">The Lord of the Isles</a></i></li>
<li>1817: <i><a href="/wiki/Harold_the_Dauntless" title="Harold the Dauntless">Harold the Dauntless</a></i></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Short_stories">Short stories</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Walter_Scott&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Short stories">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li>1827: "The Highland Widow", "The Two Drovers", and "The Surgeon's Daughter" – the 1st installment from the series <i><a href="/wiki/Chronicles_of_the_Canongate" title="Chronicles of the Canongate">Chronicles of the Canongate</a></i></li>
<li>1828: "My Aunt Margaret's Mirror", "The Tapestried Chamber", and "Death of the Laird's Jock" – from the series <i><a href="/wiki/The_Keepsake_Stories" title="The Keepsake Stories">The Keepsake Stories</a></i></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Plays">Plays</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Walter_Scott&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Plays">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li>1799: <i><a href="/wiki/G%C3%B6tz_von_Berlichingen_(Goethe)" title="Götz von Berlichingen (Goethe)">Goetz of Berlichingen, with the Iron Hand: A Tragedy</a></i> – an English-language translation of the 1773 German-language play by <a href="/wiki/Johann_Wolfgang_von_Goethe" title="Johann Wolfgang von Goethe">Johann Wolfgang von Goethe</a> entitled <i><a href="/wiki/G%C3%B6tz_von_Berlichingen_(Goethe)" title="Götz von Berlichingen (Goethe)">Götz von Berlichingen</a></i></li>
<li>1822: <i>Halidon Hill</i></li>
<li>1823: <i><a href="/wiki/MacDuff%27s_Cross" title="MacDuff&#39;s Cross">MacDuff's Cross</a></i></li>
<li>1830: <i>The Doom of Devorgoil</i></li>
<li>1830: <i>Auchindrane</i></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Non-fiction">Non-fiction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Walter_Scott&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Non-fiction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li>1796 <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://librivox.org/translations-imitations-of-german-ballads-by-sir-walter-scott/"><i>Translations &amp; imitations of German Ballads</i></a> Librivox audio</li>
<li>1814–1817: <i>The Border Antiquities of England and Scotland</i> – a work co-authored by <a href="/wiki/Luke_Clennell" title="Luke Clennell">Luke Clennell</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_Greig" title="John Greig">John Greig</a> with Scott's contribution consisting of the substantial introductory essay, originally published in 2 volumes from 1814 to 1817</li>
<li>1815–1824: <i>Essays on Chivalry, Romance, and Drama</i> – a supplement to the 1815–1824 editions of the <i><a href="/wiki/Encyclop%C3%A6dia_Britannica" title="Encyclopædia Britannica">Encyclopædia Britannica</a></i></li>
<li>1816: <i>Paul's Letters to his Kinsfolk</i></li>
<li>1819–1826: <i>Provincial Antiquities of Scotland</i></li>
<li>1821–1824: <i>Lives of the Novelists</i></li>
<li>1825–1832: <i><a href="/wiki/The_Journal_of_Sir_Walter_Scott" title="The Journal of Sir Walter Scott">The Journal of Sir Walter Scott</a></i></li>
<li>1826: <i>The Letters of Malachi Malagrowther</i></li>
<li>1827: <i>The Life of Napoleon Buonaparte</i></li>
<li>1828: <i>Religious Discourses</i></li>
<li>1828: <i>Tales of a Grandfather; Being Stories Taken from Scottish History</i> – the 1st installment from the series, <a href="/wiki/Tales_of_a_Grandfather" title="Tales of a Grandfather">Tales of a Grandfather</a></li>
<li>1829: <i>The History of Scotland: Volume I</i></li>
<li>1829: <i>Tales of a Grandfather; Being Stories Taken from Scottish History</i> – the 2nd installment from the series, <a href="/wiki/Tales_of_a_Grandfather" title="Tales of a Grandfather">Tales of a Grandfather</a></li>
<li>1830: <i>Essays on Ballad Poetry</i></li>
<li>1830: <i>The History of Scotland: Volume II</i></li>
<li>1830: <i>Tales of a Grandfather; Being Stories Taken from Scottish History</i> – the 3rd installment from the series, <a href="/wiki/Tales_of_a_Grandfather" title="Tales of a Grandfather">Tales of a Grandfather</a></li>
<li>1830: <i>Letters on Demonology and Witchcraft</i></li>
<li>1831: <i>Tales of a Grandfather; Being Stories Taken from the History of France</i> – the 4th installment from the series, <a href="/wiki/Tales_of_a_Grandfather" title="Tales of a Grandfather">Tales of a Grandfather</a></li>
<li>1893: <i>Manners, customs and history of the Highlanders of Scotland&#160;; Historical account of the clan MacGregor</i>, Glasgow.</li></ul>

<li id="cite_note-24"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-24">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.walterscott.lib.ed.ac.uk/biography/poet.html">"Scott the Poet"</a>. <li id="cite_note-25"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-25">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">See <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.walterscott.lib.ed.ac.uk/works/novels/mortality.html"><i>Old Mortality</i> on the University of Edinburgh Walter Scott website.</a></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-26"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-26">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">See Amy Witherbee, in  "Habeas Corpus: British Imaginations of Power in Walter Scott's <i>Old Mortality</i>", <i>New Literary History</i> 39 (2008): 355–67, writes:<blockquote><p>By the 1670s, conflicts between religious dissidents and the Stuart Crown had given way to a Crown policy of seizing and imprisoning opponents without recourse to the courts. In 1679, this policy of using extrajudicial imprisonments to quell rebellion finally provoked the English Parliament to pass the Act of Habeas Corpus in England. Usually translated as "produce the body", habeas corpus could be invoked by any subject to require that the king or his agents produce the body of a prisoner for adjudication before the courts. In its barest terms the Great Writ protected a subject from indefinite terms of imprisonment, from imprisonment outside the kingdom, or from imprisonment without cause. It did so by asserting the jurisdiction of the courts as superior to the executive powers of the king. The Act was thus part of a long debate within the three kingdoms about the relationship of king to law and vice versa.</p><blockquote>
</blockquote></blockquote></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-27"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-27">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Witherbee (2008), pp. 363–64. Habeas corpus had been suspended in the mid-1790s at the time of the French Revolution by <a href="/wiki/William_Pitt_the_Younger" title="William Pitt the Younger">William Pitt</a>, who had called the French declaration of human rights "monstrous". Widely publicised trials for sedition took place in Edinburgh (1793) and in London (1794) <a href="/wiki/John_Thelwall" title="John Thelwall">John Thelwall</a> and two others were charged with treason. The Scottish defendants received harsh sentences whereas the English ones were acquitted. According to historian <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://ageofrevolution.blogspot.com/2007/12/pitts-reign-of-terror.html">Anne Stott</a>: "The difference between the English and Scottish trials reflects the different legal systems. Ironically, the acquittals made the loyalist case—that England was a country where a man could have a fair trial."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-28"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-28">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Papers Relative to the Regalia of Scotland p.6 by William Bell 1829</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-29"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-29">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Papers Relative to the Regalia of Scotland p.9 by William Bell 1829</span>
</li>

Retrieved <span class="nowrap">2 May</span> 2015</span>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=Walter+Scott+Digital+Archive&amp;rft.atitle=Chronology+of+Walter+Scott%27s+life&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.walterscott.lib.ed.ac.uk%2Fbiography%2Fchronology.html&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AWalter+Scott" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446"/></span>

<table class="wikitable collapsible collapsed" style="width: 100%">
<caption>Coat of arms of Sir Walter Scott
</caption>
<tbody><tr>
<td style="width: 220px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Walter_Scott_coat_of_arms.png" class="image"><img alt="Walter Scott coat of arms.png" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Walter_Scott_coat_of_arms.png/220px-Walter_Scott_coat_of_arms.png" width="220" height="232" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Walter_Scott_coat_of_arms.png/330px-Walter_Scott_coat_of_arms.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/fd/Walter_Scott_coat_of_arms.png 2x" data-file-width="437" data-file-height="460" /></a>
</td>
<td>
<dl><dt>Crest</dt>
<dd>A nymph, in her dexter hand a sun in splendour, in her sinister a crescent (moon)</dd>
<dt>Escutcheon</dt>
<dd>Quarterly; 1st &amp; 4th or two mullets in chief and a crescent in base azure within an orle azure (Scott); 2nd &amp; 3rd or on a bend azure three mascles or, in sinister chief point a buckle or (Haliburton); escutcheon of the Hand of Ulster</dd>
<dt>Supporters</dt>
<dd>Dexter a mermaid holding in the exterior hand a mirror proper; Sinister a savage wreathed around the head and middle, holding in the exterior hand a club</dd>
<dt>Motto</dt>
<dd>(above) <i>Reparabit cornua phoebe</i> – the moon shall fill her horns again<br />(below) <i>Watch weel</i></dd></dl>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
